## Section 2.7: Special Populations & PK Adjustments

**Section Number:** 2.7

### Learning Objectives
- Explain why pharmacokinetics varies across pediatrics, pregnancy/lactation, and older adulthood.
- Identify how renal and hepatic impairment change dosing risk, monitoring needs, and clinical response.
- Apply the ADME framework to interpret therapeutic failure and delayed toxicity patterns.
- Use a structured SBAR to communicate PK concerns with trend data and timing.

### Key Takeaways
- Patient context can shift a standard dose into subtherapeutic or toxic ranges.
- Renal and hepatic changes often create delayed toxicity through accumulation.
- Low albumin increases free drug fraction for highly protein-bound medications.
- Critical illness can make oral and IM/SubQ absorption unreliable.

### Content

Special Populations & PK Adjustments

A medication order always includes a dose, a route, and a schedule. Pharmacokinetics (PK) is the reason those three details are not “one size fits all.” PK describes what the body does to the drug (ADME), and special populations are groups of patients in which one or more ADME steps is predictably different.

The nursing goal is not to memorize every drug adjustment. The nursing goal is to recognize when a standard order carries a higher risk of:

• subtherapeutic exposure (the patient receives the dose but does not get the effect)
• supratherapeutic exposure (the patient receives the dose but experiences toxicity)

Then, you apply a consistent approach:

1) Identify which ADME step is likely altered
2) Identify what data you need (vitals, weight, labs, timing, medication list)
3) Anticipate what could go wrong and how you will monitor
4) Communicate clearly and early (SBAR) when the patient’s context changes

Exam Alert (high-yield habit): When you see the words “infant,” “pregnant,” “older adult,” “kidney disease,” “liver disease,” “obesity,” “malnutrition,” or “critical illness,” your brain should automatically ask: Which part of ADME changed for this patient?

2.7.1 A Bedside Framework: “ADME + Patient Story”

Pharmacokinetics is easiest to apply when you attach it to a patient story. The same medication can behave differently because:

• Absorption changes (food, GI motility, vomiting/diarrhea, malabsorption, formulation)
• Distribution changes (body water/fat, albumin level, edema/ascites, blood flow)
• Metabolism changes (CYP activity, liver perfusion, enzyme inhibition/induction, genetics)
• Excretion changes (renal perfusion, GFR/CrCl, urine output, dialysis)

Nursing lens: “Patient story” is not a vague feeling. It is the set of conditions that change drug exposure:

• Age and development
• Pregnancy/lactation status
• Organ function (kidney/liver)
• Body composition (obesity, cachexia)
• Acute illness (sepsis, shock)
• Medication list (polypharmacy, OTCs, supplements)
• Route reliability (is the GI tract working? is perfusion adequate for IM/SubQ?)

2.7.2 Pediatrics: Neonates, Infants, Children, and Adolescents

Pediatric patients are not simply “small adults.” Many PK differences relate to development, and the clinical impact is magnified because pediatric dosing often requires calculations.

A. Absorption in pediatrics

• Gastric pH: neonates have a higher gastric pH, which can alter absorption of weak acids/bases.
• Gastric emptying and motility: more variable in infancy; can delay or unpredictably change absorption.
• IM/SubQ absorption: less reliable when perfusion is poor (ill child, cold extremities, dehydration).
• Transdermal absorption: higher risk of systemic absorption because skin is thinner and body surface area is larger relative to weight.

B. Distribution in pediatrics

• Higher total body water in infants: water-soluble drugs may distribute more widely, affecting concentration.
• Lower body fat in early life: can change distribution of lipid-soluble drugs.
• Reduced protein binding (lower albumin and different binding capacity): increases free drug fraction for highly protein-bound medications.

C. Metabolism in pediatrics

• Hepatic enzymes mature over time; some pathways are reduced in neonates.
• In some childhood periods, certain metabolic pathways become more active than in adults, potentially requiring higher weight-based doses for some drugs.

D. Excretion in pediatrics

• Renal function is immature in neonates and infants. GFR increases rapidly during the first year.
• Renally cleared drugs have a higher risk of accumulation in newborns.

Nursing implications (what you do differently)

1) Weight-based dosing is not optional.
   - Verify the current weight in kg (not lbs).
   - Confirm the dosing weight used in the order (actual vs ideal vs dosing weight).

2) Small errors become big errors.
   - A 1 mL error may be a minor variance in an adult, but it may be a clinically meaningful overdose in an infant.

3) Double-check the “chain” of calculation.
   - Verify: mg/kg → total mg → concentration (mg/mL) → volume (mL).
   - Use an oral syringe or appropriate measuring device (never “teaspoons”).

4) Monitor with development in mind.
   - A child may not report dizziness, tinnitus, or nausea clearly.
   - Use objective cues: behavior change, feeding change, respiratory pattern, urinary output.

Mini-Case 1: Gentamicin in a neonate (renal immaturity)

Situation: A 2-day-old newborn is started on gentamicin for suspected sepsis.

Key PK issue: Gentamicin is renally cleared; neonatal GFR is low and rising.

Nursing actions:
• Verify dosing weight (kg) and dosing interval.
• Confirm planned peak/trough monitoring per protocol.
• Monitor urine output trends and serum creatinine trends.
• Communicate promptly if urine output decreases or if trough is elevated.

Clinical reasoning: In neonatal dosing, “interval” is often as important as “dose.” Longer intervals may be safer when clearance is low.

Mini-Case 2: Acetaminophen in a child (dosing math + caregiver teaching)

Situation: A caregiver reports giving “a capful” of acetaminophen every 4 hours for fever.

Key PK issue: Dosing must be mg/kg and formulation-specific. “Capful” is not a dose.

Nursing actions:
• Ask: What concentration? (infant drops vs children’s liquid differ)
• Calculate maximum daily dose based on weight and product.
• Teach back: show the caregiver exactly which device to use and how to measure the dose.
• Assess for hepatotoxicity risk if doses were excessive.

Safety Callout: Pediatric medication errors often happen at home. Clear education and teach-back can prevent repeat dosing mistakes.

2.7.3 Pregnancy: Physiologic Changes and Fetal Safety

Pregnancy changes physiology in ways that directly affect PK. It also introduces a second patient: the fetus. Some exposures are acceptable in a non-pregnant adult but have unacceptable risk in pregnancy.

A. Absorption in pregnancy

• Nausea/vomiting can reduce oral absorption (missed doses, vomiting after dosing).
• Delayed gastric emptying and reflux can affect onset and tolerability.

B. Distribution in pregnancy

• Plasma volume increases significantly, increasing volume of distribution for many drugs.
• Albumin is often lower (dilutional), potentially increasing free drug fraction for highly protein-bound meds.
• Body fat increases, which may alter distribution for lipophilic drugs.

C. Metabolism in pregnancy

• Some CYP enzymes are induced, others inhibited. Changes are drug-specific.
• The same pre-pregnancy dose may become too low or too high depending on the pathway.

D. Excretion in pregnancy

• GFR increases, which can increase clearance of renally eliminated drugs.
• Some antibiotics and other renally cleared meds may require dose/interval review if therapeutic response is inadequate.

Nursing implications

• Always screen pregnancy status when clinically relevant.
• Use trusted references for pregnancy/lactation safety (institutional resources, pharmacy guidance).
• Document and communicate timing in pregnancy (trimester matters for teratogenicity risk).

Mini-Case 3: UTI in pregnancy (distribution + fetal safety)

Situation: A 20-week pregnant patient is treated for UTI. She asks if she can “just take what helped last time.”

Key PK/safety issue:
• Some antibiotics are avoided in pregnancy; others are preferred.
• Increased GFR can change drug exposure.

Nursing actions:
• Verify antibiotic choice for pregnancy safety.
• Teach the importance of completing therapy to prevent pyelonephritis (higher risk in pregnancy).
• Monitor symptom response and reinforce follow-up culture results.

Clinical reasoning: Pregnancy is not a time for “leftover medication.” Correct drug selection and adherence prevent maternal complications that also threaten the fetus.

2.7.4 Lactation: Medication Transfer into Breast Milk

Lactation considerations include:

• Whether the drug enters breast milk (drug properties)
• How much enters (dose, timing, half-life)
• Whether the infant can metabolize and eliminate the drug (infant age matters)

General nursing approach:

• Ask: “How old is the infant? Full-term or premature?”
• Ask: “Is the infant showing sedation, feeding difficulty, unusual sleepiness, rash, diarrhea?”
• If medication timing can reduce infant exposure, teach dosing right after feeding (when appropriate and safe).

Safety Callout: Many “safe” lactation medications are safe in healthy full-term infants but may not be safe for premature infants due to immature metabolism/excretion.

2.7.5 Older Adults: Aging, Polypharmacy, and Hidden Renal Decline

Older adults often have multiple simultaneous PK changes plus increased pharmacodynamic sensitivity (especially CNS-active drugs). The result is higher risk of falls, delirium, bleeding, and hypotension.

A. Absorption in older adults

Absorption may be slower, but the bigger issues are usually distribution and clearance.

B. Distribution in older adults

• ↓ total body water, ↑ body fat
• Potential ↓ albumin (especially in frailty, chronic illness)

Clinical impact:
• Lipid-soluble drugs may have prolonged action (drug stores in fat).
• Highly protein-bound drugs may have higher free fraction when albumin is low.

C. Metabolism in older adults

• Hepatic blood flow often decreases.
• Enzyme activity may be reduced in some older adults, especially with comorbid liver disease.

D. Excretion in older adults

• Renal clearance declines with age.
• Serum creatinine can look “normal” when muscle mass is low.

Nursing implications

• Look at trends, not single numbers.
  - eGFR/CrCl trend over time is more meaningful than one creatinine value.

• Watch for “accumulation patterns.”
  - The patient is fine after dose 1, sleepy after dose 3, confused after dose 5.

• Treat polypharmacy as a clinical diagnosis.
  - New drug + old drug can produce a new adverse effect.

Mini-Case 4: “Normal creatinine” and new sedation

Situation: An 82-year-old with low muscle mass has creatinine 1.0 mg/dL. After receiving standard doses of a renally cleared sedative, the patient becomes increasingly drowsy and unsteady.

Nursing actions:
• Reframe: This is likely decreased clearance, not “patient is sensitive.”
• Verify CrCl/eGFR, urine output, hydration status.
• Assess medication list for additive sedation (opioids, antihistamines, benzodiazepines).
• Hold/clarify next dose as appropriate per protocol; notify provider/pharmacy.

Clinical reasoning: Renal decline can be “invisible” in frailty. When you see a delayed or progressive effect, think accumulation.

2.7.6 Renal Impairment: When Excretion Changes First

Renal impairment can be chronic (CKD) or acute (AKI). Both can change drug exposure quickly.

A. What changes

• Reduced excretion of parent drug and/or metabolites
• Increased half-life
• Increased steady-state concentration for the same maintenance dose

B. What to assess (nursing data)

• Urine output (hourly trends in acute care)
• Serum creatinine trend, BUN trend
• eGFR/CrCl trend
• Fluid status (dehydration can worsen clearance)
• Dialysis status (timing of dialysis changes removal)

C. Common “renal clearance” red flags

• New confusion, somnolence
• Nausea/vomiting not explained by illness
• Arrhythmias
• Bleeding
• Worsening hypotension

Mini-Case 5: AKI and opioid metabolites

Situation: A patient develops AKI after dehydration. The patient is receiving an opioid that forms active metabolites cleared by the kidneys. The patient becomes more sedated over 24 hours.

Nursing actions:
• Recognize delayed toxicity risk from metabolite accumulation.
• Assess respiratory rate, oxygenation, sedation score.
• Verify renal labs and urine output trend.
• Notify provider: recommend review of opioid choice/dose/interval and consider alternatives.

Clinical reasoning: Renal impairment can change drug exposure even if the last dose was “correct.” It changes what happens next.

2.7.7 Hepatic Impairment: Metabolism, Protein Binding, and Bleeding Risk

The liver influences PK through:

• Metabolism (Phase I/II)
• Synthesis of albumin (protein binding)
• Synthesis of clotting factors (bleeding risk with anticoagulants)
• Bile production (excretion of some drugs)

A. What changes

• Reduced metabolism → higher drug levels
• Reduced albumin → increased free drug
• Altered bile flow → altered elimination for some drugs
• Prodrugs may be less effective if activation is impaired

B. What to assess

• History: hepatitis, cirrhosis, alcohol use disorder, ascites, encephalopathy
• Labs: AST/ALT (injury), bilirubin (excretion), INR (synthetic function), albumin (binding/synthetic function)

Mini-Case 6: Benzodiazepine sensitivity in cirrhosis

Situation: A patient with cirrhosis receives a benzodiazepine and becomes markedly somnolent.

Key issue: Reduced hepatic clearance + increased CNS sensitivity.

Nursing actions:
• Monitor airway, breathing, level of consciousness.
• Review medication list for additive CNS depressants.
• Notify provider; anticipate dose reduction or alternative.

Clinical reasoning: In hepatic impairment, sedation can be both a medication effect and a sign of hepatic encephalopathy. PK helps you interpret the timing and pattern.

2.7.8 Obesity and Body Composition: Distribution Is Not Just “Weight”

Obesity changes drug distribution, but the direction of change depends on the drug:

• Lipophilic drugs may have larger Vd (more fat stores).
• Hydrophilic drugs may have different distribution patterns due to changes in lean mass and body water.

Nursing implications

• Dosing weight matters (actual, ideal, adjusted). Confirm which weight the order uses.
• Monitor effect and toxicity rather than assuming “bigger patient needs bigger dose.”
• Collaborate with pharmacy for high-risk meds (anticoagulants, antibiotics with narrow therapeutic windows).

Mini-Case 7: Antibiotic dosing in obesity

Situation: A patient with obesity is treated with an antibiotic that requires target concentrations for effectiveness.

Nursing actions:
• Verify the dosing strategy and timing.
• Monitor clinical response (fever curve, WBC trend, symptoms) and adverse effects.
• Ask whether therapeutic drug monitoring is needed.

2.7.9 Malnutrition, Low Albumin, and Fluid Shifts

Low albumin increases free drug levels for highly protein-bound medications. Fluid shifts (edema, ascites) can increase apparent distribution volume for water-soluble drugs.

Nursing implications

• Highly protein-bound drugs: monitor closely for toxicity.
• Edema/ascites: distribution changes can alter onset and duration.

Clinical note: Albumin is not just a “nutrition lab.” It is a PK lab when you’re giving highly protein-bound medications.

2.7.10 Critical Illness, Sepsis, and Shock

Critical illness changes PK because perfusion and organ function can change rapidly.

• Poor gut perfusion → unreliable oral absorption
• Vasopressors and shock → reduced peripheral perfusion → unreliable IM/SubQ absorption
• Capillary leak and fluid resuscitation → distribution changes
• AKI/acute liver injury → clearance changes

Nursing implications

• Reassess route reliability: if the gut is not working, PO may not be effective.
• Watch for abrupt changes: a dose that was safe yesterday may be unsafe today if AKI develops.
• Document timing and clinical changes; communicate early.

Mini-Case 8: “The oral antibiotic isn’t working” in sepsis

Situation: A patient in early sepsis is vomiting and hypotensive. Oral antibiotics are ordered.

Nursing actions:
• Recognize: absorption is likely unreliable.
• Communicate with provider; anticipate IV route if indicated.
• Monitor response and escalate based on vital signs and clinical status.

2.7.11 Putting It Together: SBAR for PK Concerns

A consistent SBAR makes PK problems easier to solve.

S (Situation)
• “I’m concerned the patient is showing signs of accumulation/toxicity after standard dosing.”

B (Background)
• “The patient has CKD stage 3 / new AKI / cirrhosis / low albumin / pregnancy / is 2 days postpartum / is a neonate.”

A (Assessment)
• “Today’s urine output is  mL/hr, creatinine increased from  to , eGFR . After dose , the patient became .”

R (Recommendation)
• “Please review dose/interval and consider therapeutic drug monitoring or alternative medication. Should we hold the next dose until labs/levels are reviewed?”

2.7.12 Chapter Skill: A “Special Population” Safety Checklist

Before administering a high-risk medication in a special population, ask:

1) Is the route reliable today?
2) Do we have an up-to-date weight (kg) if dosing is weight-based?
3) What is the patient’s renal function trend?
4) What is the patient’s hepatic function and albumin status?
5) Did any medications start/stop recently (including OTCs/supplements)?
6) What is the monitoring plan (effect + toxicity)?
7) What is the patient teaching message (what to report, what not to do)?

Bottom line: Special populations don’t require special guessing. They require disciplined PK thinking, careful monitoring, and early communication.

EXTENDED CASEBOOK: Special Populations and PK (Addendum)

These cases expand the Chapter 2 special-populations concepts into repeatable bedside patterns. Use them as practice for identifying the ADME step that changed, the monitoring plan that prevents harm, and the communication that makes a PK concern actionable.

Casebook 2.7-1 — Neonate with rising creatinine after antibiotics

Patient snapshot
• Newborn, day of life 3; receiving renally cleared antibiotic
• Urine output decreasing over 12 hours
• Creatinine rising compared with baseline

Medication/PK focus
• Recognize that neonatal clearance is low and can worsen quickly with illness
• Prevent delayed toxicity by matching interval to clearance

ADME reasoning
• E (Excretion): immature and changing renal clearance → longer half-life
• Accumulation pattern: toxicity may appear after multiple doses

Nursing actions (what you do next)
1. Verify dosing weight in kg and confirm the ordered interval matches neonatal protocols.
2. Document exact dose times; confirm level timing (if ordered).
3. Escalate early: communicate UO and creatinine trend before next dose.
4. Monitor for medication-class-specific toxicity signs per policy.

Patient education (teach-back)
• Explain that newborn kidneys clear drugs differently than adult kidneys.
• Reassure caregivers that labs/levels are safety monitoring, not ‘extra tests.’

Debrief (what this case is teaching)
• In pediatrics, interval errors can be as dangerous as dose errors.
• Trends matter more than single numbers.

Casebook 2.7-2 — Toddler: caregiver gives ‘double’ after a missed dose

Patient snapshot
• 2-year-old with otitis media on oral antibiotic
• Caregiver reports a missed dose and then gives two doses close together

Medication/PK focus
• Accumulation risk if dosing is compressed
• Medication education and error-prevention

ADME reasoning
• A (Absorption): dose is absorbed; problem is increased frequency
• E/M: when clearance is limited, extra doses increase peak and total exposure

Nursing actions (what you do next)
1. Assess time of last doses and symptoms (GI upset, rash).
2. Provide clear instructions for missed-dose handling (do not automatically double).
3. Notify provider if dosing exceeded recommended maximum or symptoms appear.

Patient education (teach-back)
• Teach: ‘If you miss a dose, take the next scheduled dose—don’t double unless your provider tells you.’
• Use teach-back: have caregiver repeat the plan in their own words.

Debrief (what this case is teaching)
• Many toxicities in pediatrics are preventable through precise instructions and teach-back.

Casebook 2.7-3 — Pregnancy: ‘It stopped working’ late in pregnancy

Patient snapshot
• Pregnant patient in third trimester
• Reports symptom return on medication that previously worked

Medication/PK focus
• Consider physiologic changes that lower exposure (e.g., increased Vd, increased GFR)
• Avoid self-adjustment; escalate for evaluation

ADME reasoning
• D (Distribution): plasma volume increase can lower peak levels
• E (Excretion): increased GFR can increase clearance for renally eliminated drugs

Nursing actions (what you do next)
1. Assess adherence and timing; screen for vomiting/diarrhea.
2. Review whether the medication is primarily renally cleared.
3. Communicate symptom trend to provider; ask whether dose/interval requires review.

Patient education (teach-back)
• Teach not to self-increase dosing during pregnancy.
• Reinforce follow-up and monitoring expectations.

Debrief (what this case is teaching)
• Pregnancy can shift PK enough to create therapeutic failure without any ‘nonadherence.’

Casebook 2.7-4 — Lactation: premature infant and maternal medication

Patient snapshot
• Breastfeeding parent; infant born at 34 weeks
• New maternal medication started for anxiety or sleep

Medication/PK focus
• Infant clearance is immature; sedation risk may be higher
• Use evidence-based references (LactMed/institutional)

ADME reasoning
• D: drug transfer to milk depends on lipophilicity, protein binding, half-life
• Infant M/E: premature infants clear drugs slower

Nursing actions (what you do next)
1. Consult lactation medication reference or pharmacy.
2. Assess infant feeding, weight gain, and alertness.
3. Escalate if infant shows concerning symptoms.

Patient education (teach-back)
• Teach warning signs: poor feeding, unusual sleepiness, breathing changes.

Debrief (what this case is teaching)
• Lactation safety is a ‘two-patient’ PK scenario: parent + infant.

Casebook 2.7-5 — Older adult: delirium after adding an ‘innocent’ OTC

Patient snapshot
• 78-year-old with polypharmacy
• Starts OTC sleep aid; develops confusion and falls

Medication/PK focus
• Additive CNS depression and slowed clearance
• Polypharmacy as a PK/PD risk amplifier

ADME reasoning
• M: older adults may have reduced metabolism/clearance
• D/PD: CNS sensitivity increases; additive sedatives compound effects

Nursing actions (what you do next)
1. Perform a medication reconciliation including OTCs and supplements.
2. Assess delirium, vitals, and fall risk; implement safety precautions.
3. Notify provider/pharmacy to review deprescribing or alternatives.

Patient education (teach-back)
• Teach: ‘OTC does not mean harmless—tell us before adding sleep/cold medicines.’

Debrief (what this case is teaching)
• OTC products commonly drive preventable adverse events in older adults.

Casebook 2.7-6 — CKD: delayed toxicity from active metabolites

Patient snapshot
• Patient with CKD stage 4
• Receives medication with active metabolites cleared renally
• Becomes increasingly sedated over 48 hours

Medication/PK focus
• Active metabolite accumulation
• Recognize delayed toxicity patterns

ADME reasoning
• E: reduced clearance increases half-life of metabolites
• Pattern: worsening after repeated doses rather than immediate reaction

Nursing actions (what you do next)
1. Trend sedation scoring and respiratory status.
2. Review renal function trend and dosing interval.
3. Escalate for interval extension or medication change.

Patient education (teach-back)
• Teach patient to report new sleepiness, confusion, or breathing changes.

Debrief (what this case is teaching)
• Metabolites can be the real toxicity driver in renal impairment.

Casebook 2.7-7 — Dialysis: when timing changes exposure

Patient snapshot
• Patient on hemodialysis M/W/F
• Receives a renally cleared medication on dialysis day

Medication/PK focus
• Dialysis may remove some drugs; timing affects efficacy
• Coordinate administration with dialysis schedule

ADME reasoning
• E: dialysis clearance depends on protein binding, Vd, molecular size
• If medication is dialyzable, pre-dialysis dosing may be partially removed

Nursing actions (what you do next)
1. Verify dialysis schedule and document last session.
2. Consult pharmacy/provider for timing recommendations.
3. Document dose relative to dialysis and monitor response.

Patient education (teach-back)
• Teach patient why dosing schedules may differ on dialysis days.

Debrief (what this case is teaching)
• Dialysis adds a ‘fifth variable’: timing relative to extracorporeal clearance.

Casebook 2.7-8 — Cirrhosis: bleeding risk and free-drug effect

Patient snapshot
• Patient with cirrhosis, ascites, INR elevated
• Starts a highly protein-bound drug; bruising appears

Medication/PK focus
• Low albumin increases free fraction
• Synthetic dysfunction increases bleeding risk

ADME reasoning
• D: low albumin → increased free drug
• M: reduced metabolism can increase exposure

Nursing actions (what you do next)
1. Assess bruising/bleeding and review timing relative to medication start.
2. Review albumin/INR trends.
3. Escalate for dose review and risk–benefit reassessment.

Patient education (teach-back)
• Teach bleeding warning signs and when to seek urgent care.

Debrief (what this case is teaching)
• In hepatic impairment, ‘standard dose’ can behave like a higher dose.

Casebook 2.7-9 — Obesity: dosing weight and monitoring

Patient snapshot
• Patient with obesity on a weight-based medication
• Order uses ‘weight’ but does not specify which weight basis

Medication/PK focus
• Some drugs dose on actual body weight; others on ideal/adjusted weight
• Confirm strategy with pharmacy

ADME reasoning
• D: body composition changes Vd differently for lipophilic vs hydrophilic drugs
• Exposure depends on dosing-weight strategy

Nursing actions (what you do next)
1. Clarify whether actual/ideal/adjusted weight is intended.
2. Monitor therapeutic response and toxicity.
3. Document clarified strategy.

Patient education (teach-back)
• Teach patient that weight-based dosing is individualized, not ‘one formula fits all.’

Debrief (what this case is teaching)
• Avoid assumptions: obesity requires drug-specific dosing logic.

Casebook 2.7-10 — Sepsis: route failure masquerading as ‘resistance’

Patient snapshot
• Patient with sepsis, vomiting and hypotension
• Oral therapy ordered; patient worsening

Medication/PK focus
• Oral absorption unreliable due to vomiting and poor gut perfusion
• IV route may be needed for reliable exposure

ADME reasoning
• A: absorption fails when dose is not retained or gut perfusion is low
• D: fluid shifts can alter concentrations

Nursing actions (what you do next)
1. Document vomiting frequency and hemodynamic status.
2. Escalate route reliability concern early.
3. Monitor response (vitals, mental status, labs) after route change.

Patient education (teach-back)
• Explain to family that IV route is used for reliable delivery during shock.

Debrief (what this case is teaching)
• Therapeutic failure can be a delivery problem, not a drug-selection problem.

Clinical Pearls Q&A (Special Populations)

Q1. In pediatrics, what is the single most important PK habit to reduce medication harm?
A1. Name the ADME step most likely to shift in pediatrics for your patient, then verify the specific data that step depends on. For pediatrics, emphasize weight-based dosing and interval safety. The habit is: identify the risk, verify the trend data (not one number), and communicate before giving the next dose if the story changed.

Q2. In pregnancy, what is the single most important PK habit to reduce medication harm?
A2. Name the ADME step most likely to shift in pregnancy for your patient, then verify the specific data that step depends on. For pregnancy, emphasize route reliability and physiologic clearance shifts. The habit is: identify the risk, verify the trend data (not one number), and communicate before giving the next dose if the story changed.

Q3. In lactation, what is the single most important PK habit to reduce medication harm?
A3. Name the ADME step most likely to shift in lactation for your patient, then verify the specific data that step depends on. For lactation, emphasize infant maturity and exposure monitoring. The habit is: identify the risk, verify the trend data (not one number), and communicate before giving the next dose if the story changed.

Q4. In older adults, what is the single most important PK habit to reduce medication harm?
A4. Name the ADME step most likely to shift in older adults for your patient, then verify the specific data that step depends on. For older adults, emphasize polypharmacy and hidden renal decline. The habit is: identify the risk, verify the trend data (not one number), and communicate before giving the next dose if the story changed.

Q5. In renal impairment, what is the single most important PK habit to reduce medication harm?
A5. Name the ADME step most likely to shift in renal impairment for your patient, then verify the specific data that step depends on. For renal impairment, emphasize accumulation and delayed toxicity. The habit is: identify the risk, verify the trend data (not one number), and communicate before giving the next dose if the story changed.

Q6. In hepatic impairment, what is the single most important PK habit to reduce medication harm?
A6. Name the ADME step most likely to shift in hepatic impairment for your patient, then verify the specific data that step depends on. For hepatic impairment, emphasize free drug and bleeding risk. The habit is: identify the risk, verify the trend data (not one number), and communicate before giving the next dose if the story changed.

Q7. In critical illness, what is the single most important PK habit to reduce medication harm?
A7. Name the ADME step most likely to shift in critical illness for your patient, then verify the specific data that step depends on. For critical illness, emphasize route failure and rapid physiologic change. The habit is: identify the risk, verify the trend data (not one number), and communicate before giving the next dose if the story changed.

EXTENDED PRACTICE SET: Mini-Cases for Special Populations

These short mini-cases are meant to build speed and confidence. The goal is to practice the same habit repeatedly: identify the likely ADME change, verify the right data, document timing, and escalate clearly.

Mini-case: A neonate patient has declining urine output after dehydration. The patient is receiving a renally cleared medication with narrow therapeutic window. Within the next 24–72 hours, the team notices an unexpected response pattern (either reduced effect or increased adverse effects). Your PK lens should focus first on Excretion (E) / clearance. The nurse’s job is to translate that suspicion into concrete verification and communication, not to guess a new dose.

Your move: What should the nurse verify before the next dose, and what would you communicate to the provider/pharmacy team?
Model answer: Verify urine output trend, creatinine/eGFR/CrCl trend, hydration/perfusion status, and dosing interval. Document exact dose times and symptom onset. Use SBAR to state the pattern (timing + objective findings) and your hypothesis (e.g., Excretion (E) / clearance change causing accumulation or failure). Ask directly whether the dose/interval/route should be adjusted and whether levels or pharmacy review are indicated.

Mini-case: A patient with AKI patient starts a new antibiotic or antifungal. The patient is receiving a sedating medication (CNS depressant). Within the next 24–72 hours, the team notices an unexpected response pattern (either reduced effect or increased adverse effects). Your PK lens should focus first on Metabolism (M) / interactions. The nurse’s job is to translate that suspicion into concrete verification and communication, not to guess a new dose.

Your move: What should the nurse verify before the next dose, and what would you communicate to the provider/pharmacy team?
Model answer: Verify new meds/OTCs/supplements, inhibitor vs inducer timing, and changes in therapeutic effect/toxicity. Document exact dose times and symptom onset. Use SBAR to state the pattern (timing + objective findings) and your hypothesis (e.g., Metabolism (M) / interactions change causing accumulation or failure). Ask directly whether the dose/interval/route should be adjusted and whether levels or pharmacy review are indicated.

Mini-case: A older adult (frail) patient starts an OTC sleep aid or supplement. The patient is receiving a medication affected by CYP inhibition/induction. Within the next 24–72 hours, the team notices an unexpected response pattern (either reduced effect or increased adverse effects). Your PK lens should focus first on Metabolism (M) / interactions. The nurse’s job is to translate that suspicion into concrete verification and communication, not to guess a new dose.

Your move: What should the nurse verify before the next dose, and what would you communicate to the provider/pharmacy team?
Model answer: Verify new meds/OTCs/supplements, inhibitor vs inducer timing, and changes in therapeutic effect/toxicity. Document exact dose times and symptom onset. Use SBAR to state the pattern (timing + objective findings) and your hypothesis (e.g., Metabolism (M) / interactions change causing accumulation or failure). Ask directly whether the dose/interval/route should be adjusted and whether levels or pharmacy review are indicated.

Mini-case: A neonate patient starts a new antibiotic or antifungal. The patient is receiving an oral medication that binds with iron/calcium. Within the next 24–72 hours, the team notices an unexpected response pattern (either reduced effect or increased adverse effects). Your PK lens should focus first on Absorption (A). The nurse’s job is to translate that suspicion into concrete verification and communication, not to guess a new dose.

Your move: What should the nurse verify before the next dose, and what would you communicate to the provider/pharmacy team?
Model answer: Verify vomiting timing, food/tube-feed timing, spacing from iron/calcium/antacids, formulation (ER/IR), and route reliability. Document exact dose times and symptom onset. Use SBAR to state the pattern (timing + objective findings) and your hypothesis (e.g., Absorption (A) change causing accumulation or failure). Ask directly whether the dose/interval/route should be adjusted and whether levels or pharmacy review are indicated.

Mini-case: A patient with CKD patient has new edema/ascites with low albumin. The patient is receiving a medication affected by CYP inhibition/induction. Within the next 24–72 hours, the team notices an unexpected response pattern (either reduced effect or increased adverse effects). Your PK lens should focus first on Distribution (D) / free-drug fraction. The nurse’s job is to translate that suspicion into concrete verification and communication, not to guess a new dose.

Your move: What should the nurse verify before the next dose, and what would you communicate to the provider/pharmacy team?
Model answer: Verify albumin level/trend, edema/ascites, signs of toxicity, and interacting protein-bound drugs. Document exact dose times and symptom onset. Use SBAR to state the pattern (timing + objective findings) and your hypothesis (e.g., Distribution (D) / free-drug fraction change causing accumulation or failure). Ask directly whether the dose/interval/route should be adjusted and whether levels or pharmacy review are indicated.

Mini-case: A patient with cirrhosis patient develops new confusion and increasing sleepiness after repeated doses. The patient is receiving a renally cleared medication with narrow therapeutic window. Ask directly whether the dose/interval/route should be adjusted and whether levels or pharmacy review are indicated.

Mini-case: A patient with cirrhosis patient has new edema/ascites with low albumin. The patient is receiving a medication affected by CYP inhibition/induction. Within the next 24–72 hours, the team notices an unexpected response pattern (either reduced effect or increased adverse effects). Your PK lens should focus first on Distribution (D) / free-drug fraction. The nurse’s job is to translate that suspicion into concrete verification and communication, not to guess a new dose.

Your move: What should the nurse verify before the next dose, and what would you communicate to the provider/pharmacy team?

EXTENDED PRACTICE SET (v2): Mini-Cases for Special Populations

These rapid mini-cases are designed for repetition. The goal is speed: name the ADME issue, verify the next data point, and communicate the next safe step.

• Mini-case 1: A older adult becomes more sedated after each scheduled dose. ADME focus: Excretion/accumulation. Best nursing move: trend RR/sedation, check renal function trend, and request interval review.
• Mini-case 2: A patient with CKD is on vasopressors and receives IM medication. ADME focus: Absorption (IM unreliability). Best nursing move: recognize perfusion limits absorption and consider IV route.
• Mini-case 3: A newborn vomits 15 minutes after taking the dose. ADME focus: Absorption. Best nursing move: document vomiting timing, reassess route reliability, and avoid automatic re-dosing.
• Mini-case 4: A patient with cirrhosis vomits 15 minutes after taking the dose. ADME focus: Absorption. Best nursing move: document vomiting timing, reassess route reliability, and avoid automatic re-dosing.
• Mini-case 5: A older adult has low albumin and develops stronger-than-expected effects. ADME focus: Distribution/free fraction. Best nursing move: review albumin and assess for free-drug toxicity; request adjustment.
• Mini-case 6: A newborn has low albumin and develops stronger-than-expected effects. ADME focus: Distribution/free fraction. Best nursing move: review albumin and assess for free-drug toxicity; request adjustment.
• Mini-case 7: A pregnant patient vomits 15 minutes after taking the dose. ADME focus: Absorption. Best nursing move: document vomiting timing, reassess route reliability, and avoid automatic re-dosing.
• Mini-case 8: A infant starts St. John’s Wort and then loses therapeutic effect. ADME focus: Metabolism/induction. Best nursing move: recognize delayed induction and communicate need for monitoring and possible regimen change.
• Mini-case 9: A patient with CKD vomits 15 minutes after taking the dose. ADME focus: Absorption. Best nursing move: document vomiting timing, reassess route reliability, and avoid automatic re-dosing.
• Mini-case 10: A pregnant patient vomits 15 minutes after taking the dose. ADME focus: Absorption. Best nursing move: document vomiting timing, reassess route reliability, and avoid automatic re-dosing.
• Mini-case 11: A patient with cirrhosis starts St. John’s Wort and then loses therapeutic effect. ADME focus: Metabolism/induction. Best nursing move: recognize delayed induction and communicate need for monitoring and possible regimen change.
• Mini-case 12: A newborn has low albumin and develops stronger-than-expected effects. ADME focus: Distribution/free fraction. Best nursing move: review albumin and assess for free-drug toxicity; request adjustment.
• Mini-case 13: A infant becomes more sedated after each scheduled dose. ADME focus: Excretion/accumulation. Best nursing move: trend RR/sedation, check renal function trend, and request interval review.
• Mini-case 14: A critically ill patient is on vasopressors and receives IM medication. ADME focus: Absorption (IM unreliability). Best nursing move: recognize perfusion limits absorption and consider IV route.

2.7.13 Expanded Deep Dive: Translating “Special Population” Into Bedside Decisions

Special populations are easiest to manage when you translate the label into *which PK step is most likely to change* (absorption, distribution, metabolism, elimination) and what the most likely clinical failure mode will be (therapeutic failure vs delayed toxicity vs abrupt toxicity). The same adjustment logic applies whether you are caring for a 2‑day‑old neonate, a pregnant patient at 28 weeks, an older adult with frailty, or a patient in shock on multiple infusions.

This expansion adds a practical bedside framework: (1) identify the *time course* of the patient’s PK change, (2) predict the *direction* of the exposure change (↑ exposure/toxicity risk vs ↓ exposure/ineffectiveness risk), and (3) select the nursing actions that reduce harm (monitoring, timing, trend communication, and escalation).

A. The “Time Course” Lens (Why Some Problems Look Fine…Until They Don’t)

A common clinical trap is assuming that if the first few doses are tolerated, the regimen is safe. In reality, many special‑population PK changes create delayed effects:

1) Accumulation (delayed toxicity)
   - *Mechanism:* reduced clearance (renal/hepatic), longer half‑life, or saturable metabolism.
   - *What you see:* early doses appear fine; toxicity emerges after several doses as the drug approaches steady state.
   - *Nursing implication:* trends matter more than single values. A “normal” creatinine today does not guarantee stable clearance tomorrow.

2) Underexposure (delayed therapeutic failure)
   - *Mechanism:* enzyme induction (takes days), increased volume of distribution (Vd), or augmented renal clearance.
   - *What you see:* patient improves initially, then plateaus or worsens despite adherence.
   - *Nursing implication:* report timing (“symptoms returned after day 4”), not just severity.

3) Abrupt exposure shifts (sudden toxicity or failure)
   - *Mechanism:* drug–drug interactions (enzyme inhibition), dialysis initiation, stopping an inducer, major fluid shifts, or route change (IV→PO).
   - *What you see:* a sudden change in sedation, bleeding, BP, QTc, pain control, seizure frequency, or glucose.
   - *Nursing implication:* link the event to a change (“started clarithromycin yesterday,” “began CRRT this afternoon,” “TPN stopped”).

B. Pediatric Patients: “Small Adults” Is the Wrong Starting Point

Pediatrics is not one population; it is a sequence of rapidly changing physiologic states. The safest mindset is: age → organ maturity → dosing weight scalar → monitoring plan.

1) Neonates and young infants

Neonates have unique PK features:

- Absorption: gastric pH is higher; gastric emptying and intestinal motility are variable; skin is thinner (higher percutaneous absorption); IM absorption is unreliable with low muscle mass and perfusion variability.
- Distribution: higher total body water, lower body fat, and lower albumin/α1‑acid glycoprotein → altered Vd and higher free fractions for protein‑bound drugs.
- Metabolism: hepatic enzymes mature over weeks to months; pathways may be underdeveloped (risk of accumulation).
- Elimination: GFR and tubular secretion are low at birth; renal clearance increases quickly during the first weeks.

Bedside hazards to anticipate:

- Dosing‑weight confusion: birth weight vs current weight vs dosing weight. Weight can change daily (diuresis, fluid shifts). For medication safety, ensure the order reflects the correct weight and units (mg/kg vs mcg/kg/min).
- High free drug fraction: drugs that are highly protein bound may cause toxicity at “normal” total concentrations; clinical monitoring can be more informative than total levels.
- Narrow therapeutic windows: small absolute dose changes can be clinically large. A 0.1 mL measurement error may be significant.

Nursing actions:

- Verify *which weight* is used in the order (current/actual, dosing, idealized). Escalate any mismatch.
- For renally cleared drugs, monitor trends in urine output, daily weight, and creatinine (recognize creatinine may reflect maternal levels early in life).
- Use consistent timing for levels (peak/trough) when ordered; document exact administration time.

2) Children and adolescents

As children grow, clearance can exceed adult norms (especially in school‑age children), leading to underexposure at adult‑style fixed doses. Adolescents introduce new complexity: puberty‑related body composition changes, adherence variability, and interacting substances (e.g., nicotine, cannabis) that may alter metabolism or clinical response.

Nursing actions:

- Treat unexpected “nonresponse” as potentially PK‑driven, not immediately “noncompliance.”
- Ask specifically about OTC products and supplements; adolescents may not volunteer information.

C. Pregnancy: Two Patients, One Set of Labs (and Changing Physiology by Trimester)

Pregnancy changes PK through increased plasma volume, higher cardiac output, lower albumin, altered GI function, and changed renal and hepatic clearance. Many effects evolve across gestation.

Key concepts for bedside practice:

1) Distribution changes (Vd increases): hydrophilic drugs may have lower peak concentrations. Clinically this can look like “not working” even when the regimen is appropriate.

2) Protein binding decreases: lower albumin increases free fraction of highly protein‑bound drugs. Total levels may fall while free levels remain therapeutic (or increase). If a medication has available *free* drug levels, interpret those preferentially.

3) Renal clearance increases: many renally cleared drugs can be cleared faster, increasing risk of underdosing.

4) Nausea/vomiting changes absorption: oral regimens may be unreliable; repeated emesis can lead to unpredictable exposure.

5) Safety context matters: even when PK supports a dose, fetal risk, teratogenic windows, and maternal physiologic reserve influence regimen selection.

Nursing actions:

- Document gestational age and trimester when communicating to pharmacy/medical team.
- Ask about prenatal vitamins, iron, calcium, magnesium, and antacids—these can reduce absorption of some medications via chelation or altered pH.
- For medications with therapeutic drug monitoring, clarify whether target ranges differ in pregnancy and whether free levels are needed.

D. Lactation: “Is It Safe?” Becomes “How Much Gets Into Milk—and What Happens to the Infant?”

Drug transfer into breast milk depends on size, lipophilicity, protein binding, and ionization. Clinical practice focuses on risk reduction:

- Prefer drugs with short half‑lives, high protein binding, and poor oral bioavailability in the infant when alternatives exist.
- Consider infant factors: prematurity, jaundice, G6PD deficiency, and age < 2 months can increase susceptibility.

Nursing actions:

- Time doses when possible: taking a medication immediately after breastfeeding may lower infant exposure before the next feed (depends on half‑life and feeding pattern).
- Observe the infant for sedation, poor feeding, irritability, rash, or GI changes if the parent starts a new medication.
- Encourage consistent reporting: new OTC cold meds, herbal products, and “natural sleep aids” can matter.

E. Older Adults: Frailty, Not Just Age

Chronologic age is a weak predictor. Frailty (low physiologic reserve), multimorbidity, polypharmacy, and altered body composition are stronger predictors of adverse drug events.

PK themes:

- Distribution: lower total body water and increased body fat → higher peaks for water‑soluble drugs and prolonged effects of fat‑soluble drugs.
- Protein binding: lower albumin in malnutrition/inflammation increases free fractions.
- Metabolism: hepatic blood flow and enzyme activity may be reduced; first‑pass metabolism changes can increase bioavailability.
- Elimination: renal clearance often declines; serum creatinine can look “normal” despite low muscle mass.

Clinical patterns:

- “Normal dose” → delirium, falls, oversedation, orthostatic hypotension.
- Accumulation → toxicity after several days (especially sedatives, opioids, antiarrhythmics).

Nursing actions:

- Screen for delirium risk and orthostatic changes; report subtle mental‑status shifts early.
- Advocate for conservative titration (“start low, go slow”) but tie it to observed data (sedation scale, RR, BP, fall history).
- Verify renal dosing uses an appropriate estimate (and consider that creatinine may underestimate impairment in sarcopenia).

F. Obesity and Low Body Weight: Weight Scalars Change Exposure

Weight affects dosing through clearance and distribution, but not uniformly. Some drugs scale with total body weight (TBW), some with ideal body weight (IBW), and some with adjusted body weight (AdjBW). Fixed dosing can lead to underexposure in obesity; TBW dosing can overdose for drugs that do not distribute into adipose tissue.

Bedside hazards:

- Confusion between kg and lb.
- Weight in the chart not updated (fluid shifts, bed scale errors).
- Rapid titration based solely on symptoms without considering PK delay.

Nursing actions:

- Confirm the weight source (standing scale vs estimated vs bed scale) and date/time.
- For titratable infusions (e.g., heparin, insulin, sedatives), pay attention to *response lag* and avoid chasing numbers too aggressively.

G. Renal Impairment and Renal Replacement Therapy: The “Hidden” PK State Change

Renal impairment affects much more than creatinine values. It changes:

- Clearance: reduced filtration, secretion, and reabsorption.
- Volume status: fluid overload or aggressive diuresis alters Vd.
- Protein binding: uremia can displace protein‑bound drugs.
- Electrolytes and acid‑base: can alter drug ionization and cardiac risk (e.g., QT‑prolonging agents with hypokalemia/magnesemia).

Renal replacement therapies (intermittent hemodialysis, peritoneal dialysis, CRRT) add complexity:

- Dialysis can remove drugs depending on molecular size, protein binding, Vd, and dialyzer properties.
- CRRT is continuous; dosing may need to be higher than in severe renal failure without CRRT.

Nursing actions:

- Communicate the *modality and schedule* (“iHD M/W/F,” “CRRT started 14:00 with effluent 25 mL/kg/hr”). These details change dosing decisions.
- Watch for post‑dialysis “rebound” in drug levels (redistribution from tissues).
- Ensure dose timing is aligned with dialysis sessions when relevant.

H. Hepatic Impairment: Clearance, Coagulation, and Albumin Are Intertwined

Hepatic disease affects PK via reduced metabolism, altered bile flow (affects drug excretion and fat‑soluble absorption), and lower albumin production. Yet routine LFTs do not directly measure metabolic capacity.

Clinical interpretation tips:

- Elevated AST/ALT may reflect injury but not necessarily clearance capacity.
- Low albumin suggests chronic synthetic dysfunction and increases free drug fraction.
- INR elevation signals synthetic dysfunction and bleeding risk; it also complicates anticoagulant interpretation.

Nursing actions:

- Monitor for encephalopathy and sedation; hepatic impairment can magnify CNS effects.
- Report bleeding/bruising, melena, and mental status changes promptly.
- When possible, tie symptoms to dosing timeline (after dose increase? after starting inhibitor?).

I. Critical Illness: Shock, Capillary Leak, and “Augmented Renal Clearance”

ICU physiology can dramatically distort PK:

- Absorption: gut hypoperfusion reduces oral absorption; edema alters SubQ absorption.
- Distribution: capillary leak and large‑volume resuscitation increase Vd for hydrophilic drugs → lower peaks.
- Metabolism and elimination: can be decreased (organ failure) or increased (augmented renal clearance in hyperdynamic states).

Augmented renal clearance (ARC) is a common underrecognized cause of antibiotic failure in younger, trauma, or septic patients with high cardiac output and high urine output. Serum creatinine may appear normal or low while clearance is high.

Nursing actions:

- Monitor urine output and hemodynamics; communicate high outputs and improving perfusion when antibiotics appear to “not work.”
- Recognize that high doses may be needed to achieve exposure goals; therapeutic drug monitoring may be essential.

J. Drug–Drug and Drug–Food Interactions: Special Populations Multiply the Risk

Special populations rarely have a single variable. Polypharmacy, supplements, altered nutrition, and organ dysfunction make interactions more consequential.

Practical nursing checks:

- New antifungals/macrolides → enzyme inhibition (toxicity risk).
- Stopping a long‑term inducer (e.g., certain anticonvulsants, herbal inducers) → delayed toxicity over days.
- Tube feeds + certain oral meds → decreased absorption (hold feeds and flush protocols may be required).

K. A Structured “PK Concern” SBAR (Data That Makes the Team Act)

When you suspect a PK mismatch, include:

S — Situation: What is happening now (symptoms, vitals, lab changes).

B — Background: Special population factor(s): age/gestational age, renal/hepatic trends, RRT modality, obesity/frailty, critical illness.

A — Assessment: What PK step is likely altered and in what direction (↑ exposure vs ↓ exposure). Mention timing relative to doses and any recent changes.

R — Recommendation: A specific action request (level, ECG, hold dose, change route, pharmacy consult, dosing interval reassessment).

Example SBAR snippet:

- “Patient is increasingly somnolent with RR 10 after day 3 of therapy. Background: older adult with low albumin and rising creatinine. Assessment: likely accumulation with higher free fraction. Recommendation: hold next dose, obtain level if available, and request renal interval adjustment.”

      <td><strong>Pregnancy (varies by trimester)</strong></td>
      <td>↑ plasma volume/Vd; ↓ albumin; often ↑ renal clearance</td>
      <td>Lower peaks; altered total levels; underdosing risk for renally cleared meds</td>
      <td>Gestational age, vomiting/PO tolerance, BP/glucose trends, need for free drug levels when applicable</td>
    </tr>
    <tr>
      <td><strong>Lactation</strong></td>
      <td>Milk transfer depends on lipophilicity, protein binding, half-life</td>
      <td>Infant exposure (sedation/feeding issues)</td>
      <td>Infant behavior: sedation, poor feeding, irritability; parent’s new meds/OTCs/herbals</td>
    </tr>
    <tr>
      <td><strong>Older adult with frailty</strong></td>
      <td>↓ renal clearance; ↓ hepatic blood flow; ↑ fat/↓ water; ↓ albumin</td>
      <td>Falls, delirium, oversedation; delayed toxicity</td>
      <td>New confusion, gait instability, orthostasis; RR and sedation scales; creatinine trend (may look “normal”)</td>
    </tr>
    <tr>
      <td><strong>Obesity</strong></td>
      <td>↑ Vd for lipophilic drugs; dosing scalar (TBW/IBW/AdjBW) varies</td>
      <td>Over- or underdosing depending on drug</td>
      <td>Verify weight source/date; avoid unit errors; document response lag during titration</td>
    </tr>
    <tr>
      <td><strong>Renal impairment</strong></td>
      <td>↓ clearance; uremia alters binding</td>
      <td>Accumulation; electrolyte-mediated toxicity</td>
      <td>UOP, creatinine/BUN trend, K/Mg/Phos, mental status changes, bleeding (anticoagulants)</td>
    </tr>
    <tr>
      <td><strong>CRRT / Hemodialysis</strong></td>
      <td>Extracorporeal clearance varies by modality/settings</td>
      <td>Unexpected low levels or post-dialysis rebound</td>
      <td>Modality + start time + settings/effluent; timing of doses vs dialysis sessions; clinical response shifts</td>
    </tr>
    <tr>
      <td><strong>Hepatic impairment</strong></td>
      <td>↓ metabolism; ↓ albumin; altered bile flow</td>
      <td>Enhanced CNS effects; bleeding risk; higher free fraction</td>
      <td>Somnolence/encephalopathy, bruising/bleeding, albumin/INR trend, dose-response sensitivity</td>
    </tr>
    <tr>
      <td><strong>Shock / critical illness</strong></td>
      <td>↓ absorption (gut), ↑ Vd (capillary leak), variable clearance (ARC vs organ failure)</td>
      <td>Antibiotic failure or sudden toxicity after status changes</td>
      <td>Hemodynamics, pressor changes, fluid balance, UOP, route reliability (PO/IM/SubQ), need for TDM</td>
    </tr>
  </tbody>
</table>
<p><em>Use this as a pattern-recognition tool; confirm drug-specific guidance with pharmacy and institutional protocols.</em></p>

#### Block 2: Nursing Monitoring Checklist for PK Risk (Special Populations)

<h4>Before giving the dose</h4>
<ul>
  <li><strong>Confirm the scalar:</strong> correct patient weight (kg), correct dosing weight (TBW/IBW/AdjBW if specified), and correct units.</li>
  <li><strong>Confirm the context:</strong> pregnancy trimester/gestational age, lactation, age/frailty cues, renal/hepatic trends, dialysis/CRRT modality and timing.</li>
  <li><strong>Check route reliability:</strong> vomiting, ileus, shock, edema (SubQ), poor perfusion (IM) — consider whether absorption is trustworthy.</li>
  <li><strong>Scan for new interactions:</strong> new antibiotics/antifungals/macrolides, anticonvulsants, supplements/herbals, tube feeds, antacids/iron/calcium.</li>
</ul>
<h4>After giving the dose</h4>
<ul>
  <li><strong>Trend the right endpoints:</strong> vitals, sedation/respiratory status, pain score/function, mental status, bleeding, QTc (if relevant), urine output.</li>
  <li><strong>Time-stamp precisely:</strong> record exact administration time; therapeutic drug levels are only interpretable with accurate timing.</li>
  <li><strong>Watch for delayed patterns:</strong> toxicity after day 2–5 suggests accumulation; loss of effect after several days suggests induction or increased clearance.</li>
</ul>
<h4>When to escalate immediately</h4>
<ul>
  <li>New/worsening respiratory depression, severe sedation, hypotension, active bleeding, seizures, or significant arrhythmia/QTc change.</li>
  <li>Rapid renal/hepatic deterioration or initiation/change in dialysis/CRRT settings.</li>
  <li>Unexpected nonresponse in a time-sensitive condition (sepsis, status epilepticus, severe pain crisis) despite adherence.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>SBAR tip:</strong> pair the symptom with the timing (<em>“after dose #6”</em>) and the special-population factor (<em>“CRRT started today”</em>).</p>

**HTML (as rendered in site):**

  <li><strong>Confirm the context:</strong> pregnancy trimester/gestational age, lactation, age/frailty cues, renal/hepatic trends, dialysis/CRRT modality and timing.</li>
  <li><strong>Check route reliability:</strong> vomiting, ileus, shock, edema (SubQ), poor perfusion (IM) — consider whether absorption is trustworthy.</li>
  <li><strong>Scan for new interactions:</strong> new antibiotics/antifungals/macrolides, anticonvulsants, supplements/herbals, tube feeds, antacids/iron/calcium.</li>
</ul>
<h4>After giving the dose</h4>
<ul>
  <li><strong>Trend the right endpoints:</strong> vitals, sedation/respiratory status, pain score/function, mental status, bleeding, QTc (if relevant), urine output.</li>
  <li><strong>Time-stamp precisely:</strong> record exact administration time; therapeutic drug levels are only interpretable with accurate timing.</li>
  <li><strong>Watch for delayed patterns:</strong> toxicity after day 2–5 suggests accumulation; loss of effect after several days suggests induction or increased clearance.</li>
</ul>
<h4>When to escalate immediately</h4>
<ul>
  <li>New/worsening respiratory depression, severe sedation, hypotension, active bleeding, seizures, or significant arrhythmia/QTc change.</li>
