</ul>
<p><strong>SBAR tip:</strong> pair the symptom with the timing (<em>“after dose #6”</em>) and the special-population factor (<em>“CRRT started today”</em>).</p>

---

## Section 2.8: Clinical Application — Safe PK Thinking at the Bedside

**Section Number:** 2.8

### Learning Objectives
- Use a bedside PK workflow to verify safe and effective administration.
- Identify common PK-related safety traps (ER crushing, mis-timed troughs, interaction patterns).
- Document timing and trends to support accurate interpretation and dose adjustment.
- Apply patient education scripts that reflect PK principles (spacing, missed doses, warning signs).

### Key Takeaways
- PK thinking turns “something feels off” into a structured clinical message.
- Timing and trend documentation make medication safety decisions safer.
- Therapeutic failure often reflects absorption or interaction problems—not just dose size.

### Content

Clinical Application — Safe PK Thinking at the Bedside

Pharmacokinetics becomes clinically useful when it changes nursing actions. A nurse cannot rewrite a prescription, but a nurse can:

• verify that the ordered route/formulation makes sense for the patient today
• identify risk for accumulation or therapeutic failure
• monitor for expected and unexpected responses
• communicate changes early, before harm occurs

This section is a practical “PK at the bedside” toolkit. Use it to organize what you look for, what you document, and what you say when you escalate a concern.

2.8.1 A Practical PK Workflow

Think of PK as a loop rather than a one-time concept:

1) Before the dose: verify safety and likelihood of absorption
2) Give the dose correctly: route, timing, infusion rate, technique
3) After the dose: monitor response (therapeutic and adverse)
4) Interpret: ask “Which part of ADME explains what I’m seeing?”
5) Escalate and adjust the plan: dose/interval/route/monitoring/education

A. Before the dose: “Is this dose likely to be safe and effective?”

1) Confirm route and formulation

• ER/DR/EC: Do not crush unless explicitly approved.
• SL (sublingual) and buccal: do not swallow unless directed.
• Transdermal: verify patch placement and removal of old patches.
• Inhaled: verify technique; poor technique = poor dose delivery.

2) Confirm timing and spacing

• Food timing matters for some meds (absorption).
• Some meds must be spaced apart to avoid binding interactions (e.g., iron/calcium with certain antibiotics).

3) Confirm patient-specific risk (special populations)

• Pediatrics: weight in kg, dose calculation chain.
• Pregnancy/lactation: fetal/infant safety.
• Older adult: renal clearance, polypharmacy, CNS sensitivity.
• Renal/hepatic impairment: accumulation risk.

4) Confirm interaction risk

Ask explicitly about:

• OTC cold medications, antihistamines, antacids
• supplements (St. John’s Wort, ginkgo, garlic, turmeric)
• grapefruit juice (CYP3A4 interactions)

B. Giving the dose: “Do the mechanics match the physiology?”

• PO administration: is the patient vomiting? NPO? severe diarrhea? GI bleed?
• IM/SubQ: is perfusion adequate? is the site appropriate? is anticoagulation present (bleeding risk)?
• IV: confirm dilution, compatibility, infusion rate; monitor for infiltration/extravasation.

C. After the dose: “Is it working, and is it harming?”

Monitor both sides:

• Therapeutic effect: symptom relief, vital sign improvement, lab change, functional improvement
• Adverse effect: sedation, hypotension, bleeding, arrhythmias, hypoglycemia, rash, GI distress

D. Interpret using ADME

When the response is unexpected, choose a starting hypothesis:

• Too much drug (toxicity): impaired metabolism or excretion; inhibition; low albumin; dose dumping
• Too little drug (failure): poor absorption; induction; wrong technique; vomiting/diarrhea; drug binding interaction

2.8.2 Bedside Checklists That Prevent Common PK-Related Harm

Checklist 1: “Formulation safety” (the ER/IR trap)

Before administering, ask:

• Is this extended-release (ER), delayed-release (DR), enteric-coated (EC), or sustained-release (SR)?
• Is the patient ordered “crush meds”? If yes, which meds are safe to crush and which are not?
• Is there a safer alternative formulation (liquid, sprinkle capsule, IV)?

Why this matters:

Crushing an ER medication can cause dose dumping—rapid release of the full dose—leading to overdose, hypotension, respiratory depression, or severe adverse effects.

Checklist 2: “Kidney function in 30 seconds”

When the medication is renally cleared or nephrotoxic, check:

• Is urine output adequate for this patient and unit standard?
• Is creatinine rising or stable?
• What is eGFR/CrCl trend?
• Did the patient become dehydrated, hypotensive, septic, or receive contrast recently?

Nursing action when abnormal:

• Hold/clarify per protocol when safety is uncertain.
• Notify provider/pharmacy with trend data (not just “creatinine is high”).

Checklist 3: “Liver function and free drug”

When the medication is hepatically metabolized or highly protein-bound, check:

• history of cirrhosis/hepatitis
• albumin level (proxy for binding and synthetic function)
• INR and bilirubin (synthetic/excretory function)

Red flags:

• oversedation
• new confusion
• unexpected bleeding

Checklist 4: “Timing and spacing” (absorption interactions)

Common timing patterns:

• Antacids/calcium/iron can bind certain oral medications and reduce absorption.
• High-fat meals can delay or increase absorption depending on the medication.

Nursing actions:

• Ask what the patient is taking at home.
• Teach spacing rules clearly (“separate by 2 hours” is a patient education script, not a hint).

2.8.3 Case Study #1 — “Why is the patient suddenly too sleepy?” (Accumulation)

Patient: Mr. Alvarez, 76, admitted for pneumonia. History includes CKD stage 3 and mild cognitive impairment. He receives a renally cleared analgesic for pain.

Timeline:

• Dose 1: pain improves, patient remains alert.
• Dose 3 (24 hours later): patient is drowsy and needs repeated stimulation.
• Dose 5 (48 hours later): patient is confused, has shallow respirations.

Clinical question: What PK principle explains the pattern?

Guided analysis (ADME)

E (Excretion) is the most likely issue. Reduced clearance leads to accumulation. The effect is delayed and progressive.

Nursing actions

1) Assess immediately
• respiratory rate, oxygenation, sedation score
• airway safety, aspiration risk

2) Check renal data
• urine output trend
• creatinine/eGFR trend

3) Review medication list
• additive sedatives (opioids, benzodiazepines, antihistamines)

4) Communicate SBAR
• Include timing relative to doses and objective data.

5) Document clearly
• “After dose #3 at 1400, patient became drowsy; after dose #5, RR decreased to .”

Teaching point

Accumulation often looks like “the dose was fine at first.” The nurse’s power is in recognizing the trajectory and acting before the patient reaches respiratory failure.

2.8.4 Case Study #2 — “Why isn’t the medication working?” (Absorption failure)

Patient: Ms. Nguyen, 35, is treated with an oral medication that requires consistent absorption. She reports nausea and vomiting and states she “throws up soon after taking it.”

Clinical question: Is this a dose problem or an absorption problem?

Guided analysis

A (Absorption) is likely unreliable due to vomiting. The patient may not be retaining the dose long enough for absorption.

Nursing actions

• Ask timing: how soon after swallowing does vomiting occur?
• Assess hydration status.
• Consider antiemetic support per orders and communicate with provider about route change if needed.
• Teach: if vomiting occurs within a short window after taking a dose, contact the clinic for instructions (do not automatically re-dose without guidance).

Teaching point

Therapeutic failure sometimes comes from “the body never got the drug,” not “the dose was too low.”

2.8.5 Case Study #3 — “The INR changed after an antibiotic” (Metabolism and interactions)

Patient: Mr. Patel, 62, takes warfarin for atrial fibrillation with stable INR. He starts an antibiotic for a respiratory infection.

Two possible outcomes:

• INR increases: bleeding risk (possible inhibition or altered vitamin K gut flora)
• INR decreases: clot risk (possible induction)

Nursing actions

• Identify the new drug and timing.
• Communicate need for INR monitoring.
• Teach patient:
  - report bleeding signs (bruising, dark stools, nosebleeds)
  - do not start/stop OTC meds or supplements without discussing

Teaching point

Interactions are not abstract. They are clinically visible changes in response—often delayed (induction) or rapid (inhibition).

2.8.6 Therapeutic Drug Monitoring (TDM): Peaks, Troughs, and Timing

Some medications require levels because the therapeutic window is narrow.

Key definitions

• Peak: highest concentration after a dose
• Trough: lowest concentration before the next dose

Why timing matters

If a level is drawn at the wrong time, it can mislead the team into making an unsafe adjustment.

Nursing responsibilities

• Know the ordered timing for levels.
• Document exact time of dose administration and exact time of blood draw.
• If timing is off, communicate before interpretation.

Practical example

A trough drawn after the dose is given is not a trough. It will appear falsely high and may trigger an unnecessary dose reduction.

2.8.7 Patient Education Scripts That Reflect PK Thinking

A. “Do not double up” (long half-life and accumulation)

Script:

“Some medications stay in your body for a long time. Taking extra doses to catch up can cause a delayed overdose. If you miss a dose, take your next scheduled dose and call us for advice.”

B. “Spacing rules” (absorption interactions)

Script:

“Certain supplements and antacids can bind this medication and keep it from working. Take this medication at least 2 hours before or 4 hours after iron or calcium unless we tell you otherwise.”

C. “What to report” (toxicity early warning)

Script:

“If you notice unusual sleepiness, dizziness, confusion, shortness of breath, bleeding, or a new rash, call us right away. Those can be early signs that the medication level is too high for your body right now.”

2.8.8 Documentation: What Makes PK Concerns Actionable

Document with PK clarity:

• exact dose time
• route and formulation
• vital signs and assessment findings before and after
• patient report (“vomited 10 minutes after dose”)
• relevant labs and trends
• communication (who was notified, what was recommended)

Example note snippet

“2145: Administered medication X 2 mg IV over 2 minutes. Pre-dose RR 18, O2 sat 96% RA. 2205: Patient drowsy, RR 10, O2 sat 90% RA; arousable to voice. UO decreased to 20 mL/hr. Notified provider; order received to hold next dose and obtain STAT BMP.”

2.8.9 Summary: PK Is an Early-Warning System

A nurse’s PK skill is the ability to notice patterns:

• delayed worsening after repeated doses → accumulation (often clearance)
• no effect despite adherence → absorption failure, induction, technique problem
• sudden adverse effect after adding a medication → inhibition or displacement

Bottom line: PK thinking turns “something feels off” into a structured clinical message that leads to safer care.

CLINICAL TOOLKIT ADDENDUM: Communication and Documentation

When PK drives a safety issue, the difference between ‘noticed’ and ‘fixed’ is usually documentation quality and communication clarity. The goal is to give the team information that supports a safe change (dose, interval, route, monitoring) without guessing.

SBAR templates

SBAR script — accumulation concern
S: ‘I’m concerned the patient is showing delayed toxicity after repeated doses.’
B: ‘Renal/hepatic function has changed (AKI/CKD/cirrhosis).’
A: ‘After dose # at , RR decreased from  to  and sedation increased; creatinine rose from  to .’
R: ‘Can we review dose/interval and consider holding the next dose or checking levels?’

SBAR script — route reliability concern
S: ‘I’m concerned the oral route is unreliable today.’
B: ‘Patient is vomiting/hypotensive/on vasopressors.’
A: ‘Vomited within  minutes of last dose; MAP ; signs of poor perfusion.’
R: ‘Should we switch to IV/alternate route to ensure therapeutic exposure?’

Documentation checklist (high-yield)
• Exact time dose was given and route/formulation (IR vs ER).
• Exact time symptom began and objective findings (RR, O2 sat, sedation score).
• Renal trend: urine output mL/hr and creatinine/eGFR change.
• Hepatic trend: albumin/INR/bilirubin when relevant.
• New medication start/stop dates including OTCs/supplements.

Micro-scenarios (rapid pattern practice)

Micro-scenario 2.8-1 — Patch stacking

Key idea: two patches found; continuous absorption doubles delivery.

What the nurse does:
• Verify the time relationship (dose time vs symptom/level time).
• Identify which ADME step is implicated.
• Document objectively with timestamps and trends.
• Communicate with a direct recommendation (hold/adjust/redo level/switch route).

Micro-scenario 2.8-2 — Inhaler technique failure

Key idea: delivered dose is low despite adherence.

What the nurse does:
• Verify the time relationship (dose time vs symptom/level time).
• Identify which ADME step is implicated.
• Document objectively with timestamps and trends.
• Communicate with a direct recommendation (hold/adjust/redo level/switch route).

Micro-scenario 2.8-3 — Binding interaction

Key idea: iron/calcium with oral therapy reduces absorption.

What the nurse does:
• Verify the time relationship (dose time vs symptom/level time).
• Identify which ADME step is implicated.
• Document objectively with timestamps and trends.
• Communicate with a direct recommendation (hold/adjust/redo level/switch route).

Micro-scenario 2.8-4 — Infusion rate reaction

Key idea: peak exposure too high due to rapid infusion.

What the nurse does:
• Verify the time relationship (dose time vs symptom/level time).
• Identify which ADME step is implicated.
• Document objectively with timestamps and trends.
• Communicate with a direct recommendation (hold/adjust/redo level/switch route).

Micro-scenario 2.8-5 — Mis-timed trough

Key idea: false high trough leads to unsafe dose reduction.

What the nurse does:
• Verify the time relationship (dose time vs symptom/level time).
• Identify which ADME step is implicated.
• Document objectively with timestamps and trends.
• Communicate with a direct recommendation (hold/adjust/redo level/switch route).

PK COMMUNICATION LIBRARY: SBAR + Documentation Examples

PK problems are often solvable, but only if the team receives actionable information. Use these examples as language templates.

SBAR examples

Accumulation (renal)
S: ‘I’m concerned the patient is accumulating a renally cleared medication.’
B: ‘Renal function has declined since yesterday.’
A: ‘UO dropped from  to  mL/hr; creatinine rose from  to ; after dose at , sedation score increased and RR fell to .’
R: ‘Should we hold the next dose and adjust interval or check a level?’

Absorption failure (vomiting)
S: ‘I’m concerned the patient is not absorbing the oral medication.’
B: ‘Vomiting began this morning.’
A: ‘Vomits within  minutes of the dose; symptoms not improving.’
R: ‘Can we switch route or add antiemetic support? Should the dose be re-timed?’

ER crushing risk
S: ‘This is an ER medication ordered to be crushed.’
B: ‘Patient has dysphagia; order set includes crush meds.’
A: ‘Crushing ER can cause dose dumping.’
R: ‘Please change to IR/liquid/alternate route; pharmacy can recommend options.’

Interaction risk
S: ‘I’m concerned about a metabolism interaction after a new medication was started.’
B: ‘New antibiotic/supplement started  days ago.’
A: ‘Therapeutic effect changed (more toxicity / less effect) starting .’
R: ‘Can we review for CYP inhibition/induction and adjust monitoring or dosing?’

Documentation examples
• Example documentation: ‘1400 dose given PO; patient vomited at 1410. Provider notified at 1420; route changed to IV at 1500.’
• Example documentation: ‘Sedation score increased from 1 to 3 after third dose; RR decreased from 16 to 10; O2 sat 92% RA.’
• Example documentation: ‘Old patch removed from right upper arm before applying new patch to left upper back; patient instructed to avoid heating pad.’

PK COMMUNICATION LIBRARY (v2): Rapid Templates

Use these rapid templates when you need to communicate clearly under time pressure. They intentionally include the ‘what changed’ and the ‘what you want’ so the team can act quickly.

Template: ‘Hold and clarify’
‘This order may be unsafe as written because  (ER crushing / declining clearance / wrong timing). I recommend holding the next dose and clarifying with pharmacy/provider.’

Template: ‘Timing matters’
‘The dose was given at  and the level was drawn at __. This timing does/does not represent a true trough/peak.’

Template: ‘Route unreliable’
‘Given vomiting/hypoperfusion, the current route may not deliver the drug reliably. Should we switch route?’

MICRO-SCENARIO PRACTICE (v2): Bedside PK Patterns

Use these scenarios as fast practice. The target skill is not memorizing drug facts; it is recognizing the pattern, naming the ADME issue, verifying the right data, and delivering a clear, time-anchored message.

Micro-scenario 1: In long-term care, the nurse notices a patient in shock on vasopressors is ordered IM medication. Primary PK lens: Absorption (IM route reliability).

What to check (before the next dose)
- Perfusion status and vasopressor use
- Indication urgency
- Alternative IV options

What to say (one clear message)
‘IM absorption may be unreliable on vasopressors. Should we switch to IV for predictable exposure?’

Micro-scenario 2: In long-term care, the nurse notices a transdermal patch is applied but an old patch is still on the patient. Primary PK lens: Absorption (transdermal).

What to check (before the next dose)
- Presence of old patch and site documentation
- Heat sources/fever
- Symptoms of overdose

What to say (one clear message)
‘Two patches were present, which increases delivery. I removed the old patch and am monitoring for toxicity. Should we adjust the schedule or evaluate further?’

Micro-scenario 3: In outpatient clinic, the nurse notices a patient in shock on vasopressors is ordered IM medication. Primary PK lens: Absorption (IM route reliability).

What to check (before the next dose)
- Perfusion status and vasopressor use
- Indication urgency
- Alternative IV options

What to say (one clear message)
‘IM absorption may be unreliable on vasopressors. Should we switch to IV for predictable exposure?’

Micro-scenario 4: In ICU, the nurse notices a patient reports the medication ‘never works’ and is taking iron and antacids daily. Primary PK lens: Absorption.

What to check (before the next dose)
- Timing of medication relative to iron/calcium/antacids
- Food timing instructions
- Adherence and vomiting/diarrhea

What to say (one clear message)
‘This looks like an absorption interaction. The patient takes iron/antacids with the dose. Can we re-time and provide spacing instructions or choose an alternative?’

Micro-scenario 5: In outpatient clinic, the nurse notices a transdermal patch is applied but an old patch is still on the patient. Primary PK lens: Absorption (transdermal).

What to check (before the next dose)
- Presence of old patch and site documentation
- Heat sources/fever
- Symptoms of overdose

What to say (one clear message)
‘Two patches were present, which increases delivery. I removed the old patch and am monitoring for toxicity. Should we adjust the schedule or evaluate further?’

Micro-scenario 6: In postpartum unit, the nurse notices a patient in shock on vasopressors is ordered IM medication. Primary PK lens: Absorption (IM route reliability).

What to check (before the next dose)
- Perfusion status and vasopressor use
- Indication urgency
- Alternative IV options

What to say (one clear message)
‘IM absorption may be unreliable on vasopressors. Should we switch to IV for predictable exposure?’

Micro-scenario 7: In ICU, the nurse notices a patient who was stable becomes progressively more sedated after each scheduled dose. Primary PK lens: Excretion/accumulation.

What to check (before the next dose)
- Dose times and sedation/RR trend
- Urine output and eGFR/CrCl trend
- Additive sedatives or new inhibitors

What to say (one clear message)
‘I’m concerned this is accumulation: sedation is worsening after repeated doses and renal function appears to be declining. Can we review interval/dose before giving the next dose?’

Micro-scenario 8: In long-term care, the nurse notices a level labeled ‘trough’ was drawn after the dose was given. Primary PK lens: Monitoring/timing.

What to check (before the next dose)
- Exact dose time
- Exact blood draw time
- Protocol timing for trough/peak

What to say (one clear message)
‘The level is mis-timed and may be falsely high. I recommend repeating a true trough before changing the dose.’

Micro-scenario 9: In ICU, the nurse notices a patient who was stable becomes progressively more sedated after each scheduled dose. Primary PK lens: Excretion/accumulation.

What to check (before the next dose)
- Dose times and sedation/RR trend
- Urine output and eGFR/CrCl trend
- Additive sedatives or new inhibitors

What to say (one clear message)
‘I’m concerned this is accumulation: sedation is worsening after repeated doses and renal function appears to be declining. Can we review interval/dose before giving the next dose?’

Micro-scenario 10: In outpatient clinic, the nurse notices a patient who was stable becomes progressively more sedated after each scheduled dose. Primary PK lens: Excretion/accumulation.

What to check (before the next dose)
- Dose times and sedation/RR trend
- Urine output and eGFR/CrCl trend
- Additive sedatives or new inhibitors

What to say (one clear message)
‘I’m concerned this is accumulation: sedation is worsening after repeated doses and renal function appears to be declining. Can we review interval/dose before giving the next dose?’

Micro-scenario 11: In outpatient clinic, the nurse notices a transdermal patch is applied but an old patch is still on the patient. Primary PK lens: Absorption (transdermal).

What to check (before the next dose)
- Presence of old patch and site documentation
- Heat sources/fever
- Symptoms of overdose

What to say (one clear message)
‘Two patches were present, which increases delivery. I removed the old patch and am monitoring for toxicity. Should we adjust the schedule or evaluate further?’

Micro-scenario 12: In long-term care, the nurse notices a patient in shock on vasopressors is ordered IM medication. Primary PK lens: Absorption (IM route reliability).

What to check (before the next dose)
- Perfusion status and vasopressor use
- Indication urgency
- Alternative IV options

What to say (one clear message)
‘IM absorption may be unreliable on vasopressors. Should we switch to IV for predictable exposure?’

Micro-scenario 13: In postpartum unit, the nurse notices a patient reports the medication ‘never works’ and is taking iron and antacids daily. Primary PK lens: Absorption.

What to check (before the next dose)
- Timing of medication relative to iron/calcium/antacids
- Food timing instructions
- Adherence and vomiting/diarrhea

What to say (one clear message)
‘This looks like an absorption interaction. The patient takes iron/antacids with the dose. Can we re-time and provide spacing instructions or choose an alternative?’

Micro-scenario 14: In outpatient clinic, the nurse notices a patient who was stable becomes progressively more sedated after each scheduled dose. Primary PK lens: Excretion/accumulation.

What to check (before the next dose)
- Dose times and sedation/RR trend
- Urine output and eGFR/CrCl trend
- Additive sedatives or new inhibitors

What to say (one clear message)
‘I’m concerned this is accumulation: sedation is worsening after repeated doses and renal function appears to be declining. Can we review interval/dose before giving the next dose?’

Micro-scenario 15: In outpatient clinic, the nurse notices a patient who was stable becomes progressively more sedated after each scheduled dose. Primary PK lens: Excretion/accumulation.

What to check (before the next dose)
- Dose times and sedation/RR trend
- Urine output and eGFR/CrCl trend
- Additive sedatives or new inhibitors

What to say (one clear message)
‘I’m concerned this is accumulation: sedation is worsening after repeated doses and renal function appears to be declining. Can we review interval/dose before giving the next dose?’

Extended Case Scenarios (ADME-Focused)

The micro-scenarios above build pattern recognition. The following longer bedside cases show how to *string the patterns together* using ADME reasoning, time-stamps, and nursing actions. Each case is written to mimic the data a nurse actually sees: recent vitals, a few key labs, med times, and the patient’s story.

Case 1 — “It Didn’t Work” After Switching to PO (Absorption + Distribution)
Setting: Step-down unit, day 2 after ICU downgrade.
Patient: 67-year-old, pneumonia improving, now tolerating diet. BMI 41 (obesity), new NG tube discontinued.
Medication change: Analgesia changed from IV hydromorphone PRN to PO oxycodone/acetaminophen.

What the nurse notices: Two hours after the first PO dose the patient still reports 9/10 pain, HR 112, RR 24, guarding. No sedation. Nurse notes the dose was given with a full meal and the patient has active nausea and slowed GI motility (ondansetron + recent ileus). Extremities cool; edema present.

ADME lens:
- Absorption: oral onset delayed by food, nausea, slowed motility; possible reduced absorption if vomiting/ileus.
- Distribution: obesity and edema can change apparent distribution; under-perfusion can delay delivery to effect site.

Nursing actions (high yield): assess last bowel movement, emesis, and timing with food; re-check pain at predictable PO peak window; consider non-PO route if GI unreliable; SBAR: “Pain uncontrolled after PO switch; absorption likely delayed/unreliable—request reassessment of route or adjuncts.”

Case 2 — “Too Sleepy After Day 3” (Metabolism + Elimination/Accumulation)
Setting: Med-surg.
Patient: 74-year-old with UTI and rib fracture pain. Baseline CKD stage 3a.
Meds: New gabapentin and scheduled opioid added yesterday; today started trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole.

What the nurse notices: Over 24 hours the patient becomes progressively somnolent: RR drifts 18→12, O2 sat 96%→92% on room air, pupils small. Creatinine rises 1.1→1.6 mg/dL; UOP down. Family reports “he was sharp yesterday.”

ADME lens:
- Metabolism: adding inhibitors/competing pathways can raise active parent drug levels (especially with multiple CNS depressants).
- Elimination: declining renal function reduces clearance of renally eliminated drugs/metabolites → *delayed toxicity*.

Nursing actions: hold non-essential sedatives per protocol; trend RR/sedation scale and renal labs; anticipate need for dose/interval change; communicate: “Pattern fits accumulation—worsening sedation with repeated doses + rising creatinine. Request immediate regimen review before next scheduled dose.”

Case 3 — “The Level Is ‘High’… But the Patient Is Fine” (Distribution + Timing of TDM)
Setting: Telemetry.
Patient: 58-year-old treated for cellulitis; receiving vancomycin.
Event: A level is drawn and returns above target; provider is preparing to stop therapy.

What the nurse notices: The blood draw occurred 30 minutes after infusion ended, not as a true trough. Patient is afebrile, WBC trending down, renal function stable, no infusion reaction.

ADME lens:
- Distribution: right after infusion, drug is still redistributing from plasma into tissues (not at steady trough). A post-infusion sample overestimates “sustained” exposure.
- Elimination: without a correctly timed sample, you can’t infer clearance accurately.

Nursing actions: verify draw time vs. dose time; request repeat per policy (true trough or AUC protocol); document timing; message: “Current level likely reflects post-infusion distribution phase; recommend re-draw at correct time before changing therapy.”

Case 4 — “New Bleeding After Starting an Antibiotic” (Metabolism + Protein Binding)
Setting: Skilled nursing facility / rehab.
Patient: 79-year-old on warfarin for atrial fibrillation; albumin low from malnutrition.
Change: Started on an oral antibiotic for bronchitis.

What the nurse notices: INR rises from 2.3 to 4.1 in 48 hours; patient has new gum bleeding and bruising. No dose change in warfarin; diet intake poor.

ADME lens:
- Metabolism: many antibiotics inhibit warfarin metabolism and/or reduce vitamin K–producing gut flora → increased anticoagulant effect.
- Distribution: low albumin increases *free* fraction of highly protein-bound drugs (warfarin) → more effect at the same total level.

Nursing actions: assess for occult bleeding (stool/urine, neuro changes), hold dose per protocol, notify prescriber, anticipate vitamin K orders, reinforce consistent diet, and document the interaction risk.

Case 5 — “The Patch Is ‘Not Working’… Until the Fever” (Absorption: Skin Perfusion + Heat)
Setting: Home health / hospice.
Patient: 63-year-old with chronic cancer pain using a transdermal opioid patch with stable control for weeks.

What the nurse notices: During a febrile illness (T 39.1°C/102.4°F) the patient becomes more sedated and nauseated than usual; RR 10–12; family says they placed a heating pad over the patch for comfort.

ADME lens:
- Absorption: heat increases cutaneous blood flow and can accelerate transdermal absorption → sudden higher systemic levels.
- Elimination: if clearance is unchanged, the *input* increases faster than the body can eliminate → toxicity.

Nursing actions: remove external heat sources, assess respiratory status and sedation, follow emergency/hospice protocol, educate: “No heating pads/saunas/hot baths over patches,” and communicate with prescriber about dose adjustment or rescue med strategy.

Bedside Pattern Summary
Across settings, nurses prevent harm by matching *the time course* of symptoms to the most likely ADME lever:
- Fast change after a route/timing issue → Absorption
- Unexpected potency with low albumin/edema/obesity → Distribution
- Big effect after a new inhibitor/inducer → Metabolism
- Worsening after repeated doses + declining kidneys/liver → Elimination/accumulation

<tr><td><strong>1</strong> PO switch, uncontrolled pain</td><td>Absorption ± Distribution</td><td>No effect at expected PO peak; nausea/ileus/food</td><td>Verify dose/meal timing, GI function; consider non-PO route; reassess at correct window</td></tr>
<tr><td><strong>2</strong> Progressive somnolence</td><td>Elimination/Accumulation + Metabolism</td><td>Worse after repeated doses + rising creatinine</td><td>Hold/space sedatives per protocol; trend RR/sedation; request dose/interval review</td></tr>
<tr><td><strong>3</strong> ‘High’ level, stable patient</td><td>Distribution + TDM timing</td><td>Level drawn post-infusion (not trough)</td><td>Verify draw time; re-draw correctly; prevent inappropriate discontinuation</td></tr>
<tr><td><strong>4</strong> INR spike + bruising</td><td>Metabolism + Distribution (protein binding)</td><td>Bleeding within 48–72h of new antibiotic; low albumin</td><td>Assess bleeding; hold per protocol; notify; anticipate reversal plan; diet/education</td></tr>
<tr><td><strong>5</strong> Patch toxicity with fever/heat</td><td>Absorption (heat/perfusion)</td><td>Sudden sedation during fever/heating pad use</td><td>Remove heat; monitor respirations; educate; coordinate prescriber adjustment</td></tr>
</tbody></table>

#### Block 2: Post-Case Debrief Checklist (Nursing PK Reasoning)

**HTML (as rendered in site):**

<div class='checklist'>
<h4>Use after any suspected PK problem (5-minute team debrief)</h4>
<ol>
<li><strong>Timeline</strong>: What time was the last dose? When did symptoms start? Does the timing fit A, D, M, or E?</li>
<li><strong>Route reliability</strong>: Any vomiting, ileus, edema, shock, fever, heating pad, tube feeds, or missed doses?</li>
<li><strong>Patient factors</strong>: Renal trend (UOP/Cr/eGFR), hepatic trend (AST/ALT/bili), albumin, age, weight changes.</li>
<li><strong>Interaction screen</strong>: New meds, OTCs, herbals, grapefruit, alcohol, recent antibiotics/antifungals, sedatives.</li>
<li><strong>Objective endpoints</strong>: Vitals, sedation scale, pain score, glucose, INR, drug levels—were they collected at the right time?</li>
<li><strong>Action + communication</strong>: What did we hold/give/monitor? What did we recommend to the prescriber/pharmacy?</li>
<li><strong>Teach-back</strong>: What will the patient/caregiver do differently at home (timing, avoidance, warning signs)?</li>
</ol>
</div>

## Section 2.9: Key Terms Glossary

**Section Number:** 9

### Learning Objectives
- Define core pharmacokinetic parameters and explain how they relate to patient response.
- Differentiate peak, trough, half-life, clearance, and volume of distribution.
- Identify common causes of altered drug exposure (interactions, organ impairment, formulation changes).

### Key Takeaways
- Clear definitions reduce medication errors and improve communication.
- PK terms become clinically meaningful when tied to timing, monitoring, and patient context.

### Content

Key Terms Glossary (PK-focused)

Use these definitions to connect timing, patient factors, and monitoring to safer medication administration and escalation.

## Section 2.10: Review Questions & Assessment

**Section Number:** 10

### Learning Objectives
- Apply ADME concepts to common bedside medication-safety scenarios.
- Interpret delayed toxicity as possible accumulation related to clearance.
- Recognize common interaction patterns involving CYP inhibition and induction.
- Demonstrate safe nursing responses to special-population PK risk.

### Key Takeaways
- The safest answers connect patient context to ADME and identify what to verify next.
- Clear timing, trend data, and communication prevent PK-related harm.

### Content

Review Questions & Assessment

Focus on what to verify next (timing, trends, ADME, and interactions) before changing doses.
